و من بعث الى امرأته شيئا فقالت هو هدية وقال الزوج هو من المهر فالقول قوله لانه هو المملك فكان اعرف بجهة التمليك كيف وان الظاهرانه يسعى فى اسقاط الواجب قال الافي الطعام الذي يوكل فان القول قولها والمراد منه ما يكون مهيأ للاكل لانه يتعارف هدية فامافى الحنطة والشعير فالقول قوله لما بينا وقيل ما يجب عليه من الخمار والدرع وغيره ليس له ان يحتسبه من المهرلان الظاهر يكذبه والله اعلم ترجمہ اور جس شخص نے اپنی بیوی کی طرف کچھ بھیجا پھر عورت نے کہا وہ ہدیہ ہے اور شوہر نے کہا کہ وہ مہر ہے تو شوہر کا قول (مع انیمین معتبر ہوگا۔ کیونکہ شوہر ہی مالک بنانے والا ہے۔ پس وہ جہت تملیک سے زیادہ واقف ہے۔ کیسے (شوہر کا قول معتبر نہیں ہو گا ) حالانکہ ظاہر یہی ہے کہ وہ واجب کو ساقط کرنے کی کوشش کرے گا مگر اس کھانے میں جو کھایا جاتا ہے عورت کا قول ( معتبر ) ہوگا۔ اور مراد طعام ماکول سے وہ ہے جو کھا نے کیلئے تیار کیا گیا ہو۔ کیونکہ اس کا ہدیہ ہو نا متعارف ہے۔ بہر حال گندم اور جو میں شوہر کا قول ( معتبر) ہو گا۔ اس دلیل کی وجہ سے جو ہم نے بیان کی ہے اور کہا گیا کہ جو چیز شوہر پر واجب ہے یعنی اوڑھنی اور کر تا وغیرہ شوہر کیلئے جائز نہیں کہ اس کو مہر میں شمار کرے۔ اسلئے کہ ظاہر اس کی تکذیب کرتا ہے، اللہ زیادہ واقف ہے۔ تشریح صورت یہ ہے کہ شوہر نے اپنی بیوی کے پاس کوئی سامان بھیجا اور کہا کہ یہ مہر ہے اور عورت کہتی ہے کہ یہ ہدیہ ہے تو اس صورت میں شوہر کا قول مع الیمین معتبر ہوگا۔ دلیل یہ کہ شوہر مالک بنانے والا ہے۔ لہذا جہت تملیک سے وہ زیادہ واقف ہوگا کہ تملیک بطور ہدیہ ہے یا بطور ادائیگی مہر اور شوہر کے قول کو کیسے معتبر نہ مانا جائے حالانکہ ظاہر حال بھی اسی کا شاہد ہے۔ کیونکہ انسان کی پہلی کوشش یہ ہوتی ہے کہ وہ اپنے اوپر سے واجب کو ساقط کر دے نہ یہ کہ واجب باقی رہے اور تبرع کرتار ہے۔ امام محمد نے جامع صغیر میں فرمایا کہ اگر شوہر نے عورت کے پاس کھانے کی کوئی چیز بھیجی مثلاً بھونا گوشت پکا ہوا کھانا اور وہ میوے جو زیادہ دیر تک باقی نہیں رہ سکتے ان چیزور میں عورت کا قول معتبر ہو گا۔ کیونکہ ان چیزوں کا ہدیہ ہونا متعارف ہے۔ عام طور سے ان چیزوں
And whoever sent something to his wife, and she said it is a gift, and the husband said it is from the mahr, then the husband's word (with oath) is accepted, because he is the one who makes her owner, so he is more knowing of the manner of making owner. How (could it not be accepted) when the apparent is that he seeks to drop the obligation. Except regarding food that is eaten, then the woman's word is accepted. What is meant is food prepared for eating, because it is customary to be a gift. As for wheat and barley, the husband's word is accepted for the reason we mentioned. And it was said: What is obligatory upon him, like the khimar and dir' and other things, he cannot count it from the mahr, because the apparent belie him. And Allah knows best. Translation: And the person who sent something to his wife, then the woman said it is a gift and the husband said it is mahr, then the husband's word (with oath) is accepted, because the husband is the one who makes the owner, so he is more aware of the manner of ownership. How (will it not be accepted) when the apparent is that he tries to drop the obligation. Except in the case of food that is eaten, the woman's word is accepted. And the meaning of 'food that is eaten' is that which is prepared for eating, because it is customary to be a gift. However, in wheat and barley, the husband's word is accepted for the reason we stated. And it was said: What is obligatory upon him, like the khimar and dir' and others, it is not permissible for him to count it as part of the mahr, because the apparent belie him. Allah knows best. Explanation: The case is that the husband sent some goods to his wife and said this is mahr, and the woman says it is a gift. In this case, the husband's word with oath is accepted. The proof is that the husband is the one who makes the owner, so he is more aware of the manner of ownership—whether it is by way of gift or by way of payment of mahr. And how can the husband's word not be accepted, while the apparent situation also supports him, because a person's first effort is to drop the obligation from himself, not to leave the obligation and give a charitable gift. Imam Muhammad said in al-Jami' al-Saghir: If the husband sent an item of food, for example roasted meat, cooked food, and fruits that cannot remain for long, in such items the woman's word is accepted, because it is customary for these to be gifts. Generally, these items...
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This is a ruling with proof regarding a dispute between husband and wife over the nature of goods sent by the husband. The default rule: when the husband sends something to the wife, and she claims it is a gift while he claims it is part of the mahr, the husband's word (supported by oath) is taken. The reasoning: the husband is the one transferring ownership (mumallik), so he knows best his intention—whether he intended a gift or payment of a debt. Additionally, the natural tendency of a person is to try to fulfill an obligation (mahr) rather than give a voluntary gift while still being liable. The Qur'anic principle that a person's word is credible concerning his own actions supports this (al-qaulu li-man huwa al-mumallik). However, there is a significant exception: food items that are ready to eat (like roasted meat, cooked dishes, and perishable fruits). For these, the wife's word is accepted, because the custom (urf) is that such items are usually sent as gifts, not as mahr. This exception is from Imam Muhammad in al-Jami' al-Saghir. The text distinguishes between ready-to-eat food (where wife prevails) and staple grains like wheat and barley (where husband's word holds). Another important point: the husband cannot count items he is obligated to provide for maintenance (like a khimar or dir') as part of the mahr, because these are due anyway, and claiming them as mahr would contradict the apparent purpose. The ruling is based on the Hanafi principles of custom (urf) and the higher knowledge of the one performing the transaction.

Key Terms
مملك (mumallik): The one who makes another owner; in this context, the husband who gives property to the wife.
هدية (hadiyah): A gift voluntarily given without expectation of return or fulfillment of obligation.
طعام ماكول (ta'am ma'kul): Food prepared and ready to be eaten, such as cooked meals and perishable items.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a dispute over whether goods sent to the wife are mahr or gift, when does the wife's word prevail?
A) When the goods are wheat or barley.
B) When the goods are ready-to-eat food like cooked meat or perishable fruits.
C) When the husband has not paid any mahr yet.
D) When the goods include clothing like a khimar.

Key Takeaway
The husband's word is accepted for non-food items based on the mumallik principle; for ready-to-eat food, the wife's word prevails due to custom.

Nikah — Marriage
intermediate
8 min
Goods Sent by Husband: Mahr or Gift?
Ruling with proof on dispute over goods sent by husband

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule for resolving disputes over goods sent by husband to wife by identifying when the husband's or wife's claim prevails.

Core Idea
The giver knows his intent; his word counts for most goods, but custom reverses the rule for perishable food.

When a husband sends goods to his wife and they later disagree on whether it was mahr (dower) or a gift, whose statement is accepted? This passage from al-Hidayah presents a nuanced Hanafi rule: generally, the husband's word is taken because he is the giver and is presumed to be dropping an obligation. However, an exception is made for food that is typically given as a gift, where the wife's word prevails. Additionally, some scholars exclude items that are wajib upon the husband. Mastering this rule helps you understand how custom (urf) and legal principles interact in family law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the dispute
The husband sends goods to his wife. She claims they are a gift (hadiyah); he claims they are part of the mahr (dower). The qadi must decide whose claim to accept.
The dispute is over classification, not ownership — both agree the husband sent the goods.
2
Apply the general principle: the husband's word
The general rule is that the husband's word (with oath) is taken. The proof: he is the one who gave ownership (al-mumallik) and therefore knows his own intention better. Moreover, the apparent situation (zahir al-hal) is that he would seek to drop an obligatory debt (mahr) rather than voluntarily give a gift.
This is based on qiyas (rational analogy) and the principle that people prioritize obligations.
3
Identify the exception: perishable food
Imam Muhammad reports an exception: for food that is eaten (ma'kul) — like cooked dishes, roasted meat, fruits that spoil quickly — the wife's word is accepted. The reason is custom (urf): such items are usually given as gifts, not as mahr.
The exception covers items 'prepared for eating' not durable staples like wheat or barley.
4
Distinguish the exception: durable vs perishable
For durable goods like wheat, barley, honey, ghee, nuts, flour, etc., the general rule returns: husband's word. These items, even if edible, can serve as mahr because they last.
The key criterion is perishability and customary usage as a gift.
5
Consider the additional opinion on wajib items
Some scholars hold that if the husband sends items that are already obligatory upon him (e.g., basic clothing like a head-covering, shirt, trousers), he cannot count them as mahr because the apparent situation belies him. But if he sends extra items beyond the wajib, he can.
This opinion is not the mufta bihi but is noted in the text.

عورت کہتی ہے کہ یہ ہدیہ ہے تو اس صورت میں شوہر کا قول مع الیمین معتبر ہوگا۔ دلیل یہ کہ شوہر مالک بنانے والا ہے۔ لہذا جہت تملیک سے وہ زیادہ واقف ہوگا کہ تملیک بطور ہدیہ ہے یا بطور ادائیگی مہر اور شوہر جامع صغیر میں فرمایا کہ اگر شوہر نے عورت کے پاس کھانے کی کوئی چیز بھیجی مثلاً بھونا گوشت پکا ہوا کھانا اور وہ میوے جو زیادہ دیر تک باقی نہیں رہ سکتے ان چیزور میں عورت کا قول معتبر ہو گا۔ کیونکہ ان چیزوں کا ہدیہ ہونا متعارف ہے۔ عام طور سے ان چیزوں کو ہدیہ بھیجا جاتا ہے نہ کہ ادائیگی مہر کے طور پر۔ البتہ اگر عورت کے پاس گندم، جو، شہد، گھی ، اخروٹ ، بادام ، آٹا، سکر، زندہ بکری یا اور کوئی ایسی چیز بھیجی جو باقی رہنے والی ہے فساد بسرعت اس کی طرف سرایت نہیں کرتا تو اس صورت میں بھی شوہر کا قول معتبر ہو گا۔ سابقہ دلیل کی وجہ سے۔ یعنی شوہر اولاً واجب کو ساقط کرنے کی کوشش کرے گا۔ بعض کی رائے یہ ہے کہ اگر شوہر نے عورت کے پاس ایسا سامان بھیجا جو شوہر پر واجب تھا مثلاً اوڑھنی ، کرتا، پائجامہ اور گھریلو استعمال کی چیزیں تو شوہر ان کو مہر میں شمار نہیں کر سکتا ہے۔ کیونکہ ظاہر حال شوہر کیلئے مکذب ہے۔ لیکن اگر شوہر نے موزے یا چادر وغیرہ واجب سے زائد چیز بھیجی تو اسکو مہر میں شمار کر سکتا ہے۔
Whoever sent something to his wife, and she said it is a gift, and the husband said it is from the mahr, then the husband's word (with oath) is accepted because he is the one giving ownership, so he knows best the manner of giving ownership. How [could it be otherwise] when the apparent situation is that he seeks to drop an obligation? He said: Except for food that is eaten, then the wife's word is accepted. What is meant is food prepared for eating, because it is customary to give it as a gift. As for wheat and barley, the husband's word is accepted due to what we have mentioned. And it is said: What is obligatory upon him of the head-covering and shirt and other things, he is not entitled to count it from the mahr because the apparent situation belies him. And Allah knows best. Translation: And the person who sent something to his wife, then the woman said it is a gift and the husband said it is from the mahr, then the husband's word (with oath) will be accepted. Because the husband is the one who gives ownership. Thus he is more aware of the manner of giving ownership. How (will the husband's word not be accepted?) when the apparent is that he will strive to drop an obligation. Except for food that is eaten, the wife's word (will be accepted). And the meaning of food that is eaten is that which is prepared for eating. Because it is customary to give it as a gift. However, in wheat and barley, the husband's word (will be accepted) due to the proof we have mentioned. And it is said: That which is obligatory upon the husband, i.e., the head-covering and shirt etc., it is not permissible for the husband to count it as part of the mahr. Because the apparent belies him. Allah knows best. Elaboration: The scenario is that the husband sent some goods to his wife and said this is mahr, and the wife says it is a gift. In this case, the husband's word with oath will be accepted. The proof is that the husband is the one who gives ownership. Therefore, he will be more aware of the manner of ownership – whether the giving is as a gift or as payment of mahr. And how can the husband's word not be accepted when the apparent situation also witnesses in his favor? Because a person's first effort is to drop an obligation from himself, not that the obligation remains and he gives a gift. Imam Muhammad said in al-Jami' al-Saghir: If the husband sent something edible to his wife, e.g., roasted meat, cooked food, and fruits that cannot last long, in these items the wife's word will be accepted. Because it is customary to give these items as a gift. Generally, these items are sent as gifts, not as payment of mahr. However, if the husband sent wheat, barley, honey, ghee, walnuts, almonds, flour, sugar, a live goat, or any other item that is lasting and corruption does not quickly affect it, then in this case as well the husband's word will be accepted, due to the previous proof – i.e., the husband first tries to drop an obligation. Some scholars hold that if the husband sent such goods to his wife which were obligatory upon him, e.g., head-covering, shirt, trousers, and household items, then the husband cannot count them as mahr because the apparent situation belies him. But if the husband sent socks or a sheet etc., which are beyond what is obligatory, then he can count them as mahr.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage states a ruling on a dispute between husband and wife regarding goods sent by the husband. The passage is a ruling with proof, specifically an expansion (sharh) of the Hidayah text. The general principle is that the husband's word is taken (with his oath) because he is the one who gave the item and knows his intention best. The proof is both rational (the husband is the owner and knows the manner of transfer) and based on apparent custom (zahir hal). The passage then makes an exception for food that is eaten (cooked or perishable items) where the wife's word is taken, because it is customary to send such items as gifts. For durable goods like wheat, barley, etc., the husband's word returns. There is also an additional opinion (from some scholars) that items which are wajib upon the husband (like basic clothing) cannot be counted as mahr because the apparent situation contradicts it; only items beyond the wajib can be counted. This is a practical ruling in marriage (nikah) related to mahr and gifts, showing how legal principles (urf, qawl, and principle of obligation) interact.

Key Terms
مهر (mahr): The dower or bridal gift that is obligatory upon the husband to give to the wife.
هدية (hadiyah): A gift voluntarily given, not as an obligation.
قول (qawl): The statement or claim of a party in a dispute; here, whose word is accepted with oath.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
For which type of goods does the wife's word prevail in a dispute over mahr vs gift?
A) All durable goods like wheat and barley
B) Perishable food prepared for eating (e.g., cooked dishes, fruits)
C) Items that are wajib upon the husband
D) Cash and jewelry

Key Takeaway
In disputes between spouses over goods sent, the husband's word generally prevails, except for perishable food where custom dictates the wife's word.
